\pard\plain\li20\qc\sa184\sl-190\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 APPEALS}
\par
\pard\plain\li20\qc\sa103\sl-190\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 No. }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 16-460.}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Argued November 1, 2017—Decided
 January 22, }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 2018}
\par
\pard\plain\li200\qj\fi-166\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Federal district courts may
 exercise supplemental jurisdiction over state claims not otherwise within their adjudicatory authority if those claims are
 “part of the same case or controversy” as the federal claims the plaintiff asserts. 28 U. S. C. § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(a).}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  When a district court dismisses
 all claims independently qualifying for the exercise of federal jurisdiction, it ordinarily also dismisses all related state
 claims. See § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(c)(3).}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Section }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  provides that the “period
 of limitations for” refiling in state court a state claim so dismissed “shall be tolled while the claim is pending
 [in federal court] and for a period of 30 days after it is dismissed unless State law provides for a longer tolling period.”}
\par
\pard\plain\li200\qj\fi188\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 When petitioner Artis filed
 a federal-court suit against respondent District of Columbia (District), alleging a federal employment- discrimination claim
 and three allied claims under D. C. law, nearly two years remained on the applicable statute of limitations for the D. C.-law
 violations. Two and a half years later, the Federal District Court ruled against Artis on her sole federal claim and dismissed
 the D. C.-law claims under § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(c).}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Fifty-nine days after the dismissal,
 Artis refiled her state-law claims in the D. C. Superior Court, but that court dismissed them as time barred. The D. C.
 Court of Appeals affirmed, holding that § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  accorded Artis only a 30-day
 grace period to refile in state court and rejecting her argument that the word “tolled” in § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  means that the limitations
 period is suspended during the pendency of the federal suit.}
\par
\pard\plain\li200\qj\fi-166\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s63\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs32\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Held}
{\cs33\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 :}
\par
\pard\plain\li200\qj\fi188\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1. Section }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)’s}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  instruction to “toll”
 a state limitations period means to hold it in abeyance, }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 i.e.,}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  to stop the clock. Pp. }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 7-16.}

{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Syllabus}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi42\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 menced in another forum generally
 employ one of two means. First, the period of limitations may be “tolled,” }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 i.e.,}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  suspended, while the claim
 is pending elsewhere; the time clock starts running again when the tolling period ends, picking up where it left off. A
 legislature may instead elect simply to provide a grace period, permitting the statute of limitations to run while the claim
 is pending in another forum and averting the risk of a time bar by according the plaintiff a fixed period in which to refile.
 The District has identified no federal statute in which a grace-period meaning has been ascribed to the word “tolled”
 or any word similarly rooted. And the one case in which this Court used tolling language to describe a grace period, see
 }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Hardin}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  v. }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Straub,}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  490 U. S. }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 536,}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  is a feather on the scale against
 the weight of decisions in which “tolling” a statute of limitations signals stopping the clock. Pp. }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 7-11.}
\par
\pard\plain\tx716\qj\fi408\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\f0
{\listtext\pard\plain (b)HT\tab}
\ls1\ilvl0\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Considering first the ordinary
 meaning of the statutory language, § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  is phrased as a tolling provision.
 It suspends the statute of limitations both while the claim is pending in federal court and for 30 days postdismissal. Artis’
 interpretation is a natural fit with this language. The District, in contrast, reads “tolled” to mean to remove, temporarily,
 the bar that would ordinarily accompany the expiration of the limitations period. But the District offers no reason to home
 in only on the word “tolled” itself and ignore information about the }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 verb’s}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  ordinary meaning gained from
 its grammatical object, “period of limitations.” That object sheds light on what it means to “be tolled.” The
 }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 District’s}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  reading also tenders a strained
 interpretation of the phrase “period of limitations”; makes the first portion of the tolling period, the duration
 of the }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 claim’s}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  pendency in federal court,
 superfluous; and could yield an absurdity, permitting a plaintiff to refile in state court even if the limitations period
 on her claim had expired before she filed in federal court. Pp. }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 11—13.}
\par
\pard\plain\tx706\qj\fi408\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\f0
{\listtext\pard\plain (c)HT\tab}
\ls1\ilvl0\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The D. C. Court of Appeals erred
 in concluding that Congress adopted an American Law Institute (ALI) recommendation to allow refiling in state court only
 for 30 days after a dismissal. The ALI provision, like § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d),}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  established a 30-day federal
 floor on the time allowed for refiling, but it did not provide for tolling “while the [state] claim is pending” in
 federal court. Pp. }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 13-14.}

\pard\plain\tx711\qj\fi408\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\f0

\ls1\ilvl0\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The 30-day provision casts no
 large shadow on Artis’ stop-the- clock interpretation. The provision accounts for cases in which a plaintiff commenced
 a federal action close to the expiration date of the relevant state statute of limitations, by giving such a plaintiff breathing
 space to refile in state court. Adding a brief span of days to the tolling period is not unusual in stop-the-clock statutes.
 See, }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 e.g., }
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 46 U. S. C. }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §53911.}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Section }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)’s}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  proviso “unless State law
 provides for a longer tolling period” could similarly aid a plaintiff who}

{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 2.}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  The stop-the-clock interpretation
 of § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  does not present a serious
 constitutional problem. In }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Jinks}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  v. }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Richland County}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 , 538 U. S. }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 456,}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  the Court rejected an argument
 that § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  impermissibly exceeds Congress’
 authority under the Necessary and Proper Clause. }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Id.,}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 464-465.}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  The District contends that
 a stop-the-clock prescription serves “no federal purpose” that could not be served by a grace-period prescription.
 But both devices are standard, off-the- shelf means of accounting for the fact that a claim was timely pressed in another
 forum. Requiring Congress to choose one over the other would impose a tighter constraint on Congress’ discretion than
 this Court has countenanced. A concern that a stop-the-clock prescription entails a greater imposition on the States than
 a grace-period prescription may also be more theoretical than real. Finally, a stop-the- clock rule like § }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  is suited to the primary purposes
 of limitations statutes: “‘preventing surprises’” to defendants and }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 “‘barring}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  a plaintiff who has slept on
 his }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 rights.’” }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 American Pipe & Constr. Co.
 }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 v. }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Utah,}
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  414 U. S. 538, 554. Pp. 16-19.}

 Court.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi257\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The Supplemental Jurisdiction
 statute, 28 U. S. C. }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §1367,}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  enables federal district courts
 to entertain claims not otherwise within their adjudicatory authority when those claims “are so related to claims . .
 . within [federal- court competence] that they form part of the same case or controversy.” § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(a).}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Included within this supplemental
 jurisdiction are state claims brought along with federal claims arising from the same episode. When district courts dismiss
 all claims independently qualifying for the exercise of federal jurisdiction, they ordinarily dismiss as well all related
 state claims. See § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(c)(3).}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  A district court may also
 dismiss the related state claims if there is a good reason to decline jurisdiction. See § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(c)(1), (2), and (4).}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  This case concerns the time
 within which state claims so dismissed may be refiled in state court.}

{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  addressing that issue, provides:}

\pard\plain\li20\qc\sa66\sl-190\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 ARTIS }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 v.}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA}
\par
\pard\plain\li480\ri940\fi1699\sl-413\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22
\afs22
{\cs44\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Opinion of the Court }
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 State law provides for a longer
 tolling period.”}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi38\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The question presented: Does
 the word “tolled,” as used in § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d),}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  mean the state limitations
 period is suspended during the pendency of the federal suit; or does “tolled” mean that, although the state limitations
 period continues to run, a plaintiff is accorded a grace period of 30 days to refile in state court post dismissal of the
 federal case? Petitioner urges the first, or stop-the-clock, reading. Respondent urges, and the District of Columbia Court
 of Appeals adopted, the second, or grace-period, reading.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi263\sa87\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 In the case before us, plaintiff-petition
er Stephanie C. Artis refiled her state-law claims in state court 59 days after dismissal of her federal suit.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\super\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\chftn
{\footnote
\pard\plain\qj\fi230\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s55\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\super\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 :}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The nonfederal claims Artis
 asserted arose under the D. C. Code and common law; on dismissal of her federal-court suit, she refiled those claims in
 D. C. Superior Court. For the purpose at hand, District of Columbia law and courts are treated as state law and courts.
 See 28 U. S. C. § }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(e) }
{\cs17\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 (“As}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  used in this section, the term
 ‘State’ includes the District of Columbia, the Commonwealth of Puerto Rico, and any territory or possession of the
 United }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 States.”).}
}
}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Reading }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §1367(d)}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  as a grace-period prescription,
 her complaint would be time barred. Reading § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  as stopping the limitations
 clock during the pendency of the federal-court suit, her complaint would be timely. We hold that }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §1367(d)’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  instruction to “toll”
 a state limitations period means to hold it in abeyance, }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 i.e.,}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  to stop the clock. Because
 the D. C. Court of Appeals held that § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  did not stop the D. C. }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Code’s }
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 limitations clock, but merely
 provided a 30-day grace period for refiling in D. C. Superior Court, we reverse the D. C. Court of Appeals’ judgment.}

{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 In lieu of “tolling”
 or “suspending” a limitations period by pausing its progression, a legislature might elect simply to provide a grace
 period. When that mode is adopted, the statute of limitations continues to run while the claim is pending in another forum.
 But the risk of a time bar is averted by according the plaintiff a fixed period in which to refile. A federal statute of
 that genre is 28 U. S. C. }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §2415.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  That provision prescribes
 a six-year limitations period for suits seeking money damages from the United States for breach of contract. }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §2415(a).}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  The statute further provides:
 “In the event that any action ... is timely brought and is thereafter dismissed without prejudice, the action may be
 recommenced within one year after such dismissal, regardless of whether the action would otherwise then be barred by this
 section.” }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §2415(e).}
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\super\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\chftn

{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Opinion of the Court}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi37\sa155\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 sheltering claims from time
 bars during periods of a plain}
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 tiff’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  legal disability. We there
 characterized a state statute providing a one-year grace period as “tolling” or “suspend[ing]” the limitations
 period “until one year after the disability has been removed.” 490 U. S., at }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 537.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  This atypical use of “tolling”
 or “suspending” to mean something other than stopping the clock on a limitations period is a feather on the scale
 against the weight of decisions in which “tolling” a statute of limitations signals stopping the clock.}
\par
\pard\plain\qc\sa28\sl-220\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 B}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi254\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 In determining the meaning
 of a statutory provision, “we look first to its language, giving the words used their ordinary meaning.” }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Moskal}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  v. }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 United States}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 , 498 U. S. }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 103, 108 (1990)}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  (citation and internal quotation
 marks omitted). Section }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  is phrased as a tolling provision.
 It suspends the statute of limitations for two adjacent time periods: while the claim is pending in federal court and for
 30 days postdismissal. Artis urges that the phrase “shall be tolled” in § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  has the same meaning it does
 in the statutes cited }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 supra,}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at 7, n. 4. That is, the limitations
 clock stops the day the claim is filed in federal court and, 30 days postdismissal, restarts from the point at which it
 had stopped.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi254\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The District reads “tolled”
 for }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §1367(d)’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  purposes differently. To “toll,”
 the District urges, means to “remove or take away an effect.” Brief for Respondent }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 12-13.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  To “toll” a limitations
 period, then, would mean to “remov[e] the bar that ordinarily would accompany its expiration.” }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Id.,}

 its grammatical object. Just as when the object of “tolled” is “bell” or “highway traveler,” the object
 “period of limitations” sheds light on what it means to “be tolled.”}

\pard\plain\qj\fi253\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22

 by the dissent, is problematic for other reasons as well. First, it tenders a strained interpretation of the phrase “period
 of limitations.” In the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 District’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  view, “period of limitations”
 means “the effect of the period of limitations as a time bar.” See }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 id}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 ., at 18 }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 (“Section 1367(d)}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  . . . provides that The period
 of limitations’—here its effect as a time }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 bar—‘shall}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  be [removed or taken away]

{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 missed.’”}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  (alterations in original)).

{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  construction. The “effect”
 of the limitations period as a time bar, on the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 District’s}

{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Opinion of the Court}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi32\sa95\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 surdity: It could permit a
 plaintiff to refile in state court even if the limitations period on her claim had expired before she filed in federal court.
 To avoid that result, the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 District’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  proposed construction of “tolled”
 as “removed” could not mean simply “removed.” Instead, “removed” would require qualification to express
 “removed, unless the period of limitations expired before the claim was filed in federal court.” In sum, the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 District’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  interpretation maps poorly
 onto the language of § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d),}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  while Artis’ interpretation
 is a natural fit.}

{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 C}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi247\sa60\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The D. C. Court of Appeals

 primarily because it was convinced that in drafting § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d),}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Congress embraced an ALI recommendation.
 135 A. 3d, at }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 338.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Two decades before the enactment
 of § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d),}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  the ALI, in its }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1969}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Study of the Division of Jurisdiction
 Between State and Federal Courts, did recommend a 30-day grace period for refiling certain claims. The ALI proposed the
 following statutory language:}
\par
\pard\plain\li480\qj\fi-8\sa95\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22
\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 “If any claim in an action
 timely commenced in a federal court is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction over the subject matter of the claim, a new action
 on the same claim brought in another court shall not be barred by a statute of limitations that would not have barred the
 original action had it been commenced in that court, if such new action is brought in a proper court, federal or State,
 within thirty days after dismissal of the original claim has become final or within such longer period as may be available
 under applicable State law.” ALI, Study of the Division of Jurisdiction Between State and Federal Courts § }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1386(b), p. 65 (1969)}

 not only while the claim is pending in federal court, but also for 30 days thereafter. Including the 30 days within }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §1367(d)’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  tolling period accounts for
 cases in which a federal action is commenced close to the expiration date of the relevant state statute of limitations.
 In such a case, the added days give the plaintiff breathing space to refile in state court.}

 to the tolling period is not unusual in stop-the-clock statutes. In this respect, § }

 titled “Tolling of limitations period,” that if a plaintiff submits a claim for war-related vessel damage to the Secretary
 of Transportation, “the running of the limitations period for bringing a civil action is suspended until the Secretary
 denies the claim, and for 60 days thereafter.” }

 }

{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\tab The dissent, }

{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  conjures up absurdities not
 presented by this case, for the District of Columbia has no law of the kind the dissent describes. All agree that the phrase
 “unless State law provides for a longer tolling period” leaves room for a more generous state-law regime. The dissent
 posits a comparison between the duration of the federal suit, plus 30 days, and a state-law grace period. But of course,
 as the dissent recognizes, }
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 post,}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at 9, the more natural comparison
 is between the amount of time a plaintiff has left to refile, given the benefit of }
{\cs17\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §1367(d)’s}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  tolling rule, and the amount
 of time she would have to refile under the applicable state law. Should the extraordinary circumstances the dissent envisions
 in fact exist in a given case, the comparison the dissent makes would be far from inevitable.}
}

 state law claims in state court. Rather than reading the statute as generally deferring to state law judgments}

\pard\plain\li20\qc\sa97\sl-190\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs52\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs15\afs15\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 GORSUCH, }
{\cs31\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 J.,}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  dissenting}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi32\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 about the appropriate lifespan
 of state law claims brought in state courts, the Court now reads the statute as generally displacing them in favor of a
 new federal rule. Indeed, the Court today tells state courts that they must routinely disregard clearly expressed state
 law defining the appropriate length of time parties should have to sue on state law claims in state tribunals. Under the
 }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s }
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 rule, too, the disregard of
 state limitations law promises to be not only routine but substantial. The }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  approach will require state
 courts to entertain state law claims that state law deems untimely not only by weeks or months but by many years, as 24
 States, the National Conference of State Legislatures, and the Council of State Governments warn us. And the Court demands
 all this without offering any rational account why it is necessary or proper to the exercise of one of the federal }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 government’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  limited and enumerated powers.
 It may only be a small statute we are interpreting, but the result the Court reaches today represents no small intrusion
 on traditional state functions and no small departure from our foundational principles of federalism. Respectfully, I dissent.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi254\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Start with the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 statute’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  key term. Where, as here,
 a law instructs us to “toll” a limitations period, we know it may be telling us to do one of (at least) two different
 things. The dictionary informs that to “toll” means }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 “[t]o}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  take away, bar, defeat, }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 [or]}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  annul.” See 18 Oxford English
 Dictionary 204 (2d ed. }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1989); Webster’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  New International Dictionary
 }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 2662 (2d ed. 1957) }
{\cs48\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 (“[t]o}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  take away; to vacate; to }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 annul”);}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Oxford Latin Dictionary }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1947 (1982) (“tollere,”
 }
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 the Latin origin, means to
 “remove” or }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 “lift”).}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  So when a statute speaks of
 tolling a limitations period it can, naturally enough, mean }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 either}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  that the running of the limitations
 period is suspended }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 or}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  that the effect of the limitations
 period is defeated. The first understanding stops the limitations clock running until a specified event begins it running
 again: call it the stop clock approach. The second}

 }

{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 By contrast, the grace period
 approach was commonly used in cases where, as here, the plaintiff made it to court in time but arrived in the wrong court
 and had to refile in the right one. In this situation, equity }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 didn’t}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  necessarily call for suspending
 the running of the limitations period for whatever arbitrary period of time—weeks or months or years—the suit happened
 to sit in court before dismissal. }
{\cs48\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 It’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  not as if the defendant or
 uncontrollable circumstances had conspired to prevent the plaintiff from proceeding during that period. Instead, the law
 commonsensically held that in these circumstances a grace period would suffice to allow the plaintiff a brief time to find
 his way to and re file in the correct court.}

\pard\plain\qj\fi263\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Indeed, grace periods appear
 to find their roots in a common law rule known as the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 “journey’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  account” that expressly
 sought to account for and afford to a dismissed party “the number of days which }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 [he]}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  must spend in journeying to

{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 And the fact that Congress
 used a variant of the word “toll” in the second half of the sentence to refer to grace periods strongly suggests it
 did so in the first half of the sentence too. So that the first phrase “shall be }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 tolled}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  while the claim is pending
 and for a period of 30 days” should be understood to extend a grace period of 30 days after dismissal much as the second
 phrase }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 “tolling}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  period” is understood to
 refer the reader to parallel state law grace periods affording short periods for refiling after dismissal.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi257\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The alternative reading endorsed
 by the Court today extends too little respect to }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Congress’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  competency as drafter. It
 asks us to assume the legislature was so garbled in its expression that it switched the meaning of the term “toll”
 halfway through a single sentence without telling anyone. It asks us to conclude that when Congress spoke of the period
 “tolled” in the first part of the sentence it meant to refer (unambiguously, no less) to a stop clock approach even
 though it used the term “tolling period” to refer to existing state law grace periods in the second part of the sentence.
 The }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 statute’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  text drops no hint of such
 a silent switch and }
{\cs48\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 it’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  a lexicographical leap our
 traditional rules of statutory interpretation warn against.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi257\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 That, though, represents just
 the beginning of the textual troubles with the approach the Court adopts. Consider next the fact that section }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  tells us to apply its federal
 tolling rule “unless” state law provides a “longer tolling period.” In this way, the statute asks us to compare
 the length of the state “tolling period” with the length of the federal “tolling period” set by section }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1367(d)}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  and apply the longer one.
 See }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 ante,}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at 16 (courts apply the federal
 rule if “the state-law tolling period will not be longer than }
{\cs48\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §1367(d)’s”).}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  The equation }
{\cs48\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 we’re}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  asked to perform is straightforward
 and sensible if we understand both the state and federal “tolling periods” discussed in}
\sect

\pard\plain\li20\qc\sa242\sl-190\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 ARTIS }
{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 v.}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA}
\par
\pard\plain\li20\qc\sa97\sl-190\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs52\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs15\afs15\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 GORSUCH, }
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 J., dissenting}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi31\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 this statute as grace periods.
 We simply pick the longer grace period: is it the federal 30 day period or one provided by state law?}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi251\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 By contrast, the equation is
 anything but straightforward or sensible under the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  approach. The Court tells
 us that, under its reading of the statute, the federal “tolling period” is the “duration of the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 claim’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  pendency in federal court”
 plus 30 days. See }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 ante,}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at 12, 15. So the decision
 whether to apply the federal or the state tolling period turns not on the sensible question which would afford the plaintiff
 more time to refile but instead on the happenstance of how long the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 plaintiff’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  claim sat in federal court
 before dismissal. Under the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  interpretation, we apply the
 federal stop clock rule if, but only if, the time the case happened to linger in federal court before dismissal (plus 30
 days) is longer than the relevant state grace period. But to state the test is to see it is a nonsense—one we would not
 lightly attribute to any rational drafter, let alone Congress.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi251\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Consider some examples of the
 absurdities that follow from the apples-to-oranges comparison the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  test requires. Say state law
 provides a 5 year statute of limitations and a 1 year grace period for refiling. The plaintiff files in federal court one
 day before the statute of limitations expires. The litigation in federal court lasts 1 year. Under the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  view, the federal “tolling
 period” would be 1 year plus 30 days—the time the claim was pending in federal court plus 30 days after dismissal.
 That period is longer than the state tolling period of 1 year and so the federal tolling rule, not the state rule, controls—leaving
 the plaintiff only 31 days to refile her claim after dismissal even though state law would have allowed a full year.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi251\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 That may be curious enough,
 but curiouser it gets. Now suppose the litigation in federal court lasts only 10 months. That makes the federal tolling
 period only 11 months (10 months plus 30 days). Under the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  view,}
\sect

{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 state law now provides a longer
 tolling period (1 year) and the litigant gets a full year to refile in state court instead of 31 days. No one has offered
 a reason why the happenstance of how long the federal litigation lasted should determine how much time a litigant has to
 refile in state court. Yet that is what the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}

{\cs48\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 it’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  easy enough to imagine the
 rule the Court really wants to adopt today: it would like to afford litigants as a matter of federal law the benefit of
 a stop clock approach whenever doing so would yield more time to refile than the }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 state’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  grace period would permit.
 But to accomplish so much we would need a very different statute than the one we have. We would need to be able to compare
 the relevant state law grace “tolling period” }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 not }
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 with the federal “tolling
 period” as the statute says but with the }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 amount of time left under the
 relevant state limitations period on the date the plaintiff filed her federal suit.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  The problem is, no one has
 even hinted how we might lawfully superimpose all those italicized words (entirely of our own devise) onto the statutory
 text.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\super\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\chftn
{\footnote
\pard\plain\tx271\qj\fi226\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s55\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\super\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\chftn}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\tab In footnote 12 of its opinion,
 the Court suggests that a comparison between state and federal tolling periods may not be “inevitable” and that in
 “extraordinary circumstances” like those discussed above a comparison between the state tolling period and the time
 left on the clock before the federal filing might prove “more natural.” }
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Ante,}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at 16, n. 12. But even here
 the Court does not attempt to explain how the latter comparison might be fairly extracted from the statutory text—let
 alone only in “extraordinary circumstances.”}

\pard\plain\qj\fi265\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 There are still more textual
 clues that we have lost our way today. Congress spoke of the federal tolling period as embracing “30 days after . . .
 dismissal.” That language sounds like and fits with a traditional grace period or }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 journey’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  account approach. As }

{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  seen, grace periods often
 speak about affording parties some short period of time after dismissal to refile their claims. Meanwhile, this}

{\cs34\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 v.}
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA}
\par
\pard\plain\li20\qc\sa94\sl-190\slmult0\wrapdefault\s57\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs52\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs15\afs15\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 GORSUCH, }
{\cs28\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 J., dissenting}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi33\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 language proves no small challenge
 to square with a stop clock approach. Generally we say a clock is stopped due to the onset of a particular event like a
 disability: }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 something }
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 usually causes the stopping
 of the clock and when that }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 something}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  goes away, the clock restarts.
 Here, the Court says, the clock stops once a claim is pending in federal court. Yet it }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 doesn’t}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  restart when that something—the
 }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 claim’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  pendency—goes away but
 instead waits another 30 days before ticking again. All without any apparent reason for the additional delay.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi247\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 This case illustrates the oddity.
 The petitioner filed her suit in federal court with 23 months remaining on the three year statute of limitations. The case
 remained in federal district court for nearly three years before dismissal. Under the grace period approach the 30 day provision
 does just as it appears, providing petitioner with 30 days to journey to and refile in the correct court. Under the stop
 clock approach, though, the statute affords the petitioner 23 months }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 plus}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  a random 30 days more to refile.
 Indeed, on the stop clock approach the }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 only}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  work the 30 day period is
 even imaginably left to do comes in cases where the plaintiff filed her federal suit at the very end of the limitations
 period. And if }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 that’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  the only problem Congress
 sought to address, it chose a mighty murky way to do it, for the parties point to not a single stop clock provision in all
 of federal law that includes language anything like this. All while (again) this language fits hand in glove with every
 grace period statute known.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\super\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\chftn
{\footnote
\pard\plain\qj\fi35\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s55\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 meanwhile, the stop clock approach
 construes “period of limitations” to refer to the “running of the period of limitations” that is tolled or taken
 away. The question is which inference is more persuasive. And in light of the dual kinds of tolling the law has long recognized,
 as well as the textual and contextual clues before us (some still to be discussed), the better answer is clear.}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi218\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s55\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Second}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 , the Court complains that the
 grace period interpretation renders “superfluous” the phrase “while the claim is pending.” }
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Ante,}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at 12. But the phrase does
 important work under the grace period approach, ensuring that the expiration of limitations period does not take effect
 while the claim is pending in federal court. Indeed and somewhat paradoxically, the Court itself implicitly recognizes that
 the language }
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 does}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  real work when it suggests
 (in its next sentence no less) that the grace period approach could “yield an absurdity” by working to revive a claim
 that has already expired before it is brought in federal court. }
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Ante,}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 12—13.}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  There is of course no absurdity
 in it, for the term “while the claim is pending” does the important work of addressing that very concern, preventing
 the expiration of the statute of limitations from taking effect while the claim is pending even as the language also and
 sensibly permits the statute of limitations to take effect if it expires before the plaintiff files his claim in federal
 court.}
}
}

\pard\plain\qj\fi47\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 abled the litigant from reaching
 a court in the first place and all he must do is journey from the old court to the new one. We }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 don’t}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  assume Congress strips replanted
 statutes of their soil, and we should not assume Congress displaced so much tradition in favor of something comparatively
 foreign.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\super\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\chftn
{\footnote
\pard\plain\tx281\qj\fi228\sl-216\slmult0\wrapdefault\s55\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\super\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\chftn}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0\tab The Court dismisses this
 “history lesson” on the ground that it }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 doesn’t}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  know if Congress had “the
 ancient common-law ... in mind.” }
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Ante,}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at 14, n. 11. But respect for
 }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Congress’s}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  competency means we presume
 it knows the substance of the state laws it expressly incorporates into its statutes and the common law against which it
 operates. See }
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 supra,}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  at 11. When the Court turns
 to offer its own competing contextual evidence, it observes that a stop clock approach can be found in many other places
 in the U. S. Code. See }
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 ante}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 , at 7, and n. 4, }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 15-16. }
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 But it turns out the }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  stop clock examples often involve
 situations where some disability prevents the plaintiff from proceeding to court. See, }
{\cs18\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 e.g.,}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  26 U. S. C. }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 §6213(f)(1)}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  (limitations period for filing
 a petition in the Tax Court “shall be suspended for the period during which the debtor is prohibited . . . from filing
 a }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 petition”); §6503(a)(l)}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  (limitations period on making
 an assessment shall “be suspended for the period during which the Secretary is prohibited from making the assess}
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 ment”).}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  Notably, not one of the }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  examples purports to address
 a situation like the one we face: where the plaintiff has proven able to come to court but merely chosen the wrong one.
 The }
{\cs16\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs15\lang1033\langfe1033\b\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs19\afs19\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  own contextual evidence, then,
 serves to illustrate just how unusual and out of place a stop clock approach would be here.}
}
}
\par
\pard\plain\qj\fi262\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  reformation of the statute
 introduces another problem still—one of significantly greater magnitude yet. In our constitutional structure, the federal
 government’s powers are supposed to be “few and defined,” while the powers reserved to the States “remain .
 . . numerous and indefinite.” The Federalist No. 45, p. 328 (B. Wright ed. }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 1961)}

 Trying to imagine how the }

{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\super\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 a}

 pending in federal court” it may be necessary to impose a short grace period. But how is it necessary to do anything
 more than that, like consult the happenstance of how long the federal court took to dismiss the case and then tack an equivalent
 number of months or years onto state law limitations periods? What federal interest could }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 that}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  even plausibly serve? The
 Court does not and cannot attempt an answer}

 [d] this right in virtue of their sovereignty.” }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Ibid.}

\pard\plain\qj\fi252\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The decision today gives short
 shrift to these traditional interests. Just consider how differently the two approaches treat States when it comes to one
 of their most “important of sovereign rights.” Under a grace period approach, Congress simply fills a void, for the
 great bulk of States provide for grace periods of 30 days or longer; only a few States }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 don’t}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  allow that much or }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 don’t}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  speak to the question. See
 n. 3, }
{\cs30\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs23\afs23\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 supra.}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  So on the grace period account,
 Congress provides a modest backstop consistent with existing state law. By contrast, under the stop clock interpretation,
 state law grace periods are displaced whenever the federal litigation (plus those odd 30 days) happens to be longer than
 the state law grace period. And that, of course, is sure to happen often, for federal litigation is no quick business and
 state law grace periods often are. Any time federal litigation (plus, again, 30 days) lasts}

 approach will usurp its place.}

\pard\plain\qj\fi252\sl-264\slmult0\wrapdefault\s62\aspalpha\aspnum\faauto\adjustright\cgrid\f0\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 The stop clock approach, then,
 ensures that traditional state law judgments about the appropriate lifespan of state law claims will be routinely displaced—and
 displaced in favor of nothing more than a fortuity (the time a claim sits in federal court) that bears no rational relationship
 to any federal interest. The }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  approach forces state courts
 to entertain routinely state law claims that the state legislatures treat as no claims at all. And it forces state courts
 to entertain claims that }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 aren’t}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  just stale by days or weeks
 under state law, but stale by months or even many years too. So, for example, take a plaintiff who files suit in federal
 court shortly after a six year state law limitations period begins running and the litigation lasts six years before }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 it’s}
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0  finally dismissed. Under the
 }
{\cs41\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 Court’s }
{\cs29\lang1033\langfe1033\b0\i0\ul0\strike0\scaps0\fs22\afs22\charscalex100\expndtw0\cf1\dn0 approach, federal law will
 now promise the plaintiff nearly six years more (plus those stray 30 days again) to refile his claim in state court. Neither
 is this illustration fiction; it is drawn from the facts of }
